ocean between Africa, Asia, Australia and Antarctica (or the Southern Ocean)</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th class="fn org" href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-1994-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Indian Ocean</b> is the third largest of the world\'s <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceanic</a> divisions, covering 70,560,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (27,240,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) (approximately 20% of the <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>\'s surface).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rasul_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rasul-3">[3]</a></sup> It is bounded by <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> on the north, on the west by <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, on the east by <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and on the south by the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> or, depending on definition, by <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Resized-inu-afrmap-4004357-recto-master.png"><img alt="" 2x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A 1658 naval map by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Janssonius" title="Jan Janssonius">Janssonius</a> depicting the Indian Ocean, India and Arabia.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>The Indian Ocean is named after India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarperIndianOcean_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-HarperIndianOcean-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\nCalled the Sindhu Mahasagara or the great sea of the Sindhu by the Ancient Indians, this ocean has been variously called Hindu Ocean, Indic Ocean, etc. in various languages. The Indian Ocean was also known earlier as the Eastern Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HarperIndianOcean_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-HarperIndianOcean-5">[5]</a></sup> The term was still in use during the mid-18th century.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans#Indian_Ocean" title="Borders of the oceans">borders of the Indian Ocean</a>, as delineated by the <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> in 1953 included the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> but not the marginal seas along the northern rim, but in 2000 the IHO delimited the Southern Ocean separately, which removed waters south of 60&#176;S from the Indian Ocean, but included the northern marginal seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO1953_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHO1953-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO2002_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHO2002-8">[8]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meridional" title="Meridional">Meridionally</a>, the Indian Ocean is delimited from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> by the <a href="/wiki/20th_meridian_east" title="20th meridian east">20&#176; east meridian</a>, running south from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a>, and from the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> by the meridian of 146&#176;49\'E, running south from the southernmost point of <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>. The northernmost extent of the Indian Ocean (including marginal seas) is approximately <a href="/wiki/30th_parallel_north" title="30th parallel north">30&#176; north</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO2002_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-IHO2002-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Indian Ocean covers 70,560,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (27,240,000&#160;sq&#160;mi), including the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and the Persian Gulf but excluding the Southern Ocean, or 19.5% of the world\'s oceans; its volume is 264,000,000&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (63,000,000&#160;cu&#160;mi) or 19.8% of the world\'s oceans\' volume; it has an average depth of 3,741&#160;m (12,274&#160;ft) and a maximum depth of 7,906&#160;m (25,938&#160;ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eakins_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eakins-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ocean\'s continental shelves are narrow, averaging 200 kilometres (120&#160;mi) in width. An exception is found off Australia\'s western coast, where the shelf width exceeds 1,000 kilometres (620&#160;mi). The average depth of the ocean is 3,890&#160;m (12,762&#160;ft). Its deepest point is <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Trench" title="Sunda Trench">Sunda Trench</a> at a depth of 7,450&#160;m (24,442&#160;ft); <a href="/wiki/Diamantina_Deep" title="Diamantina Deep">Diamantina Deep</a> has a depth of 7,079&#160;m (23,225&#160;ft). North of <a href="/wiki/50th_parallel_south" title="50th parallel south">50&#176; south latitude</a>, 86% of the main basin is covered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a> sediments, of which more than half is <a href="/wiki/Globigerina" title="Globigerina">globigerina</a> ooze. The remaining 14% is layered with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrigenous" title="Terrigenous">terrigenous</a> sediments. Glacial outwash dominates the extreme southern latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The major choke points include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bab_el_Mandeb" title="Bab el Mandeb">Bab el Mandeb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Strait of Hormuz</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lombok_Strait" title="Lombok Strait">Lombok Strait</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Palk_Strait" title="Palk Strait">Palk Strait</a>. Seas include the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Sea" title="Andaman Sea">Andaman Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laccadive_Sea" title="Laccadive Sea">Laccadive Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mannar" title="Gulf of Mannar">Gulf of Mannar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Oman" title="Gulf of Oman">Gulf of Oman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and other tributary water bodies. The Indian Ocean is artificially connected to the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, which is accessible via the Red Sea. All of the Indian Ocean is in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern Hemisphere</a> and the centre of the Eastern Hemisphere, the <a href="/wiki/90th_meridian_east" title="90th meridian east">90th meridian east</a>, passes through the <a href="/wiki/Ninety_East_Ridge" title="Ninety East Ridge">Ninety East Ridge</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marginal_seas">Marginal seas</span></h3>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_seas" title="Marginal seas">Marginal seas</a>, gulfs, bays and straits of the Indian Ocean include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHO2002_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-IHO2002-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Andaman_Sea" title="Andaman Sea">Andaman Sea</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Australian_Bight" title="Great Australian Bight">Great Australian Bight</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mannar" title="Gulf of Mannar">Gulf of Mannar</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aden" title="Gulf of Aden">Gulf of Aden</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Aqaba" title="Gulf of Aqaba">Gulf of Aqaba</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tadjoura" title="Gulf of Tadjoura">Gulf of Tadjoura</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bahrain" title="Gulf of Bahrain">Gulf of Bahrain</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Carpentaria" title="Gulf of Carpentaria">Gulf of Carpentaria</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Kutch" title="Gulf of Kutch">Gulf of Kutch</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Khambat" title="Gulf of Khambat">Gulf of Khambat</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Oman" title="Gulf of Oman">Gulf of Oman</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Indonesia#Indonesian_Seaway" title="Geology of Indonesia">Indonesian Seaway</a> (including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malacca_Strait" title="Malacca Strait">Malacca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sunda_Strait" title="Sunda Strait">Sunda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Straits</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Laccadive_Sea" title="Laccadive Sea">Laccadive Sea</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Palk_Strait" title="Palk Strait">Palk Strait</a> connecting Arabian Sea and Bay of Bengal</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Guardafui_Channel" title="Guardafui Channel">Guardafui Channel</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Zanj" title="Sea of Zanj">Sea of Zanj</a></li>\n<li>Strait of <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a> connecting Arabian Sea</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Strait of Hormuz</a> connecting Persian Gulf</li></ul></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h2>\n<p>The climate north of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> is affected by a <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> climate. Strong north-east winds blow from October until April; from May until October south and west winds prevail. In the Arabian Sea the violent Monsoon brings rain to the Indian subcontinent. In the southern hemisphere, the winds are generally milder, but summer storms near Mauritius can be severe. When the monsoon winds change, cyclones sometimes strike the shores of the Arabian Sea and the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Indian Ocean is the warmest ocean in the world. Long-term ocean temperature records show a rapid, continuous warming in the Indian Ocean, at about 0.7&#8211;1.2&#160;&#176;C (1.3&#8211;2.2&#160;&#176;F) during 1901&#8211;2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">[10]</a></sup> Indian Ocean warming is the largest among the tropical oceans, and about 3 times faster than the warming observed in the Pacific. Research indicates that human induced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_warming" title="Greenhouse warming">greenhouse warming</a>, and changes in the frequency and magnitude of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Ni&#241;o">El Ni&#241;o</a> events are a trigger to this strong warming in the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanography">Oceanography</span></h2>\n<p>Among the few large rivers flowing into the Indian Ocean are the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jubba_River" title="Jubba River">Jubba</a> in Africa; the <a href="/wiki/Shatt_al-Arab" title="Shatt al-Arab">Shatt al-Arab</a> in the Persian Gulf; the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_River" title="Ganges River">Ganges</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmaputra" title="Brahmaputra">Brahmaputra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Godavari" title="Godavari">Godavari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krishna_River" title="Krishna River">Krishna</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narmada" title="Narmada">Narmada</a> on the Indian Subcontinent; and <a href="/wiki/Irrawaddy_River" title="Irrawaddy River">Irrawaddy</a> in Myanmar.\n</p><p>The ocean\'s <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">currents</a> are mainly controlled by the monsoon. Two large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gyre" title="Gyre">gyres</a>, one in the northern hemisphere flowing clockwise and one south of the equator moving anticlockwise (including the <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Current" title="Agulhas Current">Agulhas Current</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Return_Current" title="Agulhas Return Current">Agulhas Return Current</a>), constitute the dominant flow pattern. During the winter monsoon (November&#8211;February), however, circulation is reversed north of 30&#176;S and winds are weakened during winter and the transitional periods between the monsoons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Deep water circulation is controlled primarily by inflows from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, and Antarctic currents. North of <a href="/wiki/20th_parallel_south" title="20th parallel south">20&#176; south latitude</a> the minimum surface temperature is 22&#160;&#176;C (72&#160;&#176;F), exceeding 28&#160;&#176;C (82&#160;&#176;F) to the east. Southward of <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_south" title="40th parallel south">40&#176; south latitude</a>, temperatures drop quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Precipitation and evaporation leads to <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> variation in all oceans, and in the Indian Ocean salinity variations are driven by: (1) river inflow mainly from the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>, (2) fresher water from the <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Throughflow" title="Indonesian Throughflow">Indonesian Throughflow</a>; and (3) saltier water from the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Han_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Han-12">[12]</a></sup>\nSurface water salinity ranges from 32 to 37 parts per 1000, the highest occurring in the Arabian Sea and in a belt between southern Africa and south-western Australia. Pack ice and icebergs are found throughout the year south of about <a href="/wiki/65th_parallel_south" title="65th parallel south">65&#176; south latitude</a>. The average northern limit of icebergs is <a href="/wiki/45th_parallel_south" title="45th parallel south">45&#176; south latitude</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span></h2>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="791" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png/180px-Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png/270px-Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png/360px-Opening_of_western_Indian_Ocean_150_Ma.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png/180px-Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png/270px-Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png/360px-Opening_of_eastern_Indian_Ocean_40_Ma.png 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Left: The oldest ocean floor of the Indian Ocean formed c. 150&#160;Ma when the Indian Subcontinent and Madagascar broke-up from Africa.  Right: The India&#8211;Asia collision c. 40&#160;Ma completed the closure of the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> (grey areas north of India).</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main category: <a href="/wiki/Category:Landforms_of_the_Indian_Ocean" title="Category:Landforms of the Indian Ocean">Landforms of the Indian Ocean</a></div>\n<p>As the youngest of the major oceans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stow_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stow-13">[13]</a></sup> the Indian Ocean has active spreading ridges that are part of the worldwide system of <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a>.  In the Indian Ocean these spreading ridges meet at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodrigues_Triple_Point" title="Rodrigues Triple Point">Rodrigues Triple Point</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Central_Indian_Ridge" title="Central Indian Ridge">Central Indian Ridge</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Carlsberg_Ridge" title="Carlsberg Ridge">Carlsberg Ridge</a>, separating the <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African Plate</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Indian_Ridge" title="Southwest Indian Ridge">Southwest Indian Ridge</a> separating the African Plate from the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Indian_Ridge" title="Southeast Indian Ridge">Southeast Indian Ridge</a> separating the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian Plate</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Plate" title="Antarctic Plate">Antarctic Plate</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Central_Indian_Ridge" title="Central Indian Ridge">Central</a> Ridge runs north on the in-between across of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are only two trenches in the Indian Ocean: the 6,000&#160;km (3,700&#160;mi)-long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Java_Trench" title="Java Trench">Java Trench</a> between Java and the Sunda Trench and the 900&#160;km (560&#160;mi)-long <a href="/wiki/Makran_Trench" title="Makran Trench">Makran Trench</a> south of Iran and Pakistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A series of ridges and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seamount_chain" title="Seamount chain">seamount chains</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspots</a> pass over the Indian Ocean.  The <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union_hotspot" title="R&#233;union hotspot">R&#233;union hotspot</a> (active 70&#8211;40 million years ago) connects <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9union" title="R&#233;union">R&#233;union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mascarene_Plateau" title="Mascarene Plateau">Mascarene Plateau</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Chagos-Laccadive_Ridge" title="Chagos-Laccadive Ridge">Chagos-Laccadive Ridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Traps" title="Deccan Traps">Deccan Traps</a> in north-western India; the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_hotspot" title="Kerguelen hotspot">Kerguelen hotspot</a> (100&#8211;35 million years ago) connects the <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Islands" title="Kerguelen Islands">Kerguelen Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kerguelen_Plateau" title="Kerguelen Plateau">Kerguelen Plateau</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ninety_East_Ridge" title="Ninety East Ridge">Ninety East Ridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rajmahal_Traps" title="Rajmahal Traps">Rajmahal Traps</a> in north-eastern India; the Marion hotspot (100&#8211;70 million years ago) possibly connects <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Islands" title="Prince Edward Islands">Prince Edward Islands</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Eighty_Five_East_Ridge" title="Eighty Five East Ridge">Eighty Five East Ridge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muller_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muller-15">[15]</a></sup> These hotspot tracks have been broken by the still active spreading ridges mentioned above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The opening of the Indian Ocean began <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 156&#160;<abbr title="million year ago">Ma</abbr> when Africa separated from East <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>. The Indian Subcontinent began to separate from Australia-Antarctica 135-125 Ma and as the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> north of India began to close 118-84&#160;Ma the Indian Ocean opened behind it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chatt-etal-2013_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Chatt-etal-2013-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_life">Marine life</span></h2>\n<p>Among the tropical oceans, the western Indian Ocean hosts one of the largest concentration of <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> blooms in summer, due to the strong <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds. The monsoonal wind forcing leads to a strong coastal and open ocean <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a>, which introduces nutrients into the upper zones where sufficient light is available for photosynthesis and phytoplankton production. These phytoplankton blooms support the marine ecosystem, as the base of the marine food web, and eventually the larger fish species. The Indian Ocean accounts for the second largest share of the most economically valuable <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> catch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO-2016_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-2016-16">[16]</a></sup> Its fish are of great and growing importance to the bordering countries for domestic consumption and export. Fishing fleets from Russia, Japan, South Korea, and Taiwan also exploit the Indian Ocean, mainly for <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a> and tuna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWFB-1994_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-1994-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Research indicates that increasing ocean temperatures are taking a toll on the marine ecosystem. A study on the phytoplankton changes in the Indian Ocean indicates a decline of up to 20% in the marine phytoplankton in the Indian Ocean, during the past six decades. The tuna catch rates have also declined abruptly during the past half century, mostly due to increased industrial fisheries, with the ocean warming adding further stress to the fish species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roxy-2016_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roxy-2016-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Endangered marine species include the <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugong</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_(mammal)" title="Seal (mammal)">seals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turtle" title="Turtle">turtles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWFB-1994_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-1994-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_garbage_patch" title="Indian Ocean garbage patch">Indian Ocean garbage patch</a> was discovered in 2010 covering at least 5&#160;million square kilometres (1.9&#160;million square miles). Riding the southern <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_Gyre" title="Indian Ocean Gyre">Indian Ocean Gyre</a>, this vortex of <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic garbage</a> constantly circulates the ocean from Australia to Africa, down the <a href="/wiki/Mozambique_Channel" title="Mozambique Channel">Mozambique Channel</a>, and back to Australia in a period of six years, except for debris that get indefinitely stuck in the centre of the gyre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The garbage patch in the Indian Ocean will, according to a 2012 study, decrease in size after several decades to vanish completely over centuries.  Over several millennia, however, the global system of garbage patches will accumulate in the North Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, UK researchers from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southampton_University" title="Southampton University">Southampton University</a> identified six new animal species at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vents" title="Hydrothermal vents">hydrothermal vents</a> beneath the Indian Ocean. These new species were a "Hoff" crab, a "giant peltospirid" snail, a whelk-like snail, a limpet, a scaleworm and a polychaete worm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_settlements">First settlements</span></h3>\n<p>The history of the Indian Ocean is marked by maritime trade; cultural and commercial exchange probably date back at least seven thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-21">[21]</a></sup>  During this period, independent, short-distance oversea communications along its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">littoral</a> margins have evolved into an all-embracing network.  The d&#233;but of this network was not the achievement of a centralised or advanced civilisation but of local and regional exchange in the Persian Gulf, the Red Sea, and Arabian Sea.  Sherds of <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid</a> (2500&#8211;500&#160;BCE) pottery have been found in the western Gulf at <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Dilmun</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>; traces of exchange between this trading centre and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.  The <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> traded grain, pottery, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitumen" title="Bitumen">bitumen</a> (used for <a href="/wiki/Reed_boat" title="Reed boat">reed boats</a>) for copper, stone, timber, tin, dates, onions, and pearls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp19-22_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp19-22-22">[22]</a></sup>\nCoast-bound vessels transported goods between the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a> (2600&#8211;1900&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>) in the Indian subcontinent (modern-day Pakistan and Northwest India) and the Persian Gulf and Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i>, an <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandrian</a> guide to the world beyond the Red Sea&#160;&#8212; including Africa and India&#160;&#8212; from the first century CE, not only gives insights into trade in the region but also shows that Roman and Greek sailors had already gained knowledge about the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-21">[21]</a></sup>  The contemporaneous settlement of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_people" title="Austronesian people">Austronesian</a> sailors shows that the littoral margins of the Indian Ocean were being both well-populated and regularly traversed at least by this time.  Albeit the monsoon must have been common knowledge in the Indian Ocean for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alpers-pp1-2_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alpers-pp1-2-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The world\'s earliest civilizations in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (beginning with <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>), <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> (beginning with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a>), which began along the valleys of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>-<a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a> rivers respectively, all developed around the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup> Civilizations soon arose in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a> (beginning with <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a>) and later in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (beginning with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Funan</a>).\n</p><p>During Egypt\'s first dynasty (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3000&#160;BCE), sailors were sent out onto its waters, journeying to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punt_(region)" title="Punt (region)">Punt</a>, thought to be part of present-day Somalia. Returning ships brought gold and slaves. The earliest known maritime trade between Mesopotamia and the Indus Valley (c. 2500 BCE) was conducted along the Indian Ocean. <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> of the late 3rd millennium BCE may have entered the area, but no settlements resulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Indian Ocean\'s relatively calmer waters opened the areas bordering it to trade earlier than the Atlantic or Pacific oceans. The powerful monsoons also meant ships could easily sail west early in the season, then wait a few months and return eastwards. This allowed ancient Indonesian peoples to cross the Indian Ocean to settle in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> around 1&#160;CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitzpatrick_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzpatrick-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Discovery">Age of Discovery</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2868" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/300px-Silk_route.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/450px-Silk_route.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Silk_route.jpg/600px-Silk_route.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The economically important <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> was blocked from Europe by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1453 with the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. This spurred exploration, and a new sea route around Africa was found, triggering the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 2nd or 1st century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cyzicus" title="Eudoxus of Cyzicus">Eudoxus of Cyzicus</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek</a> to cross the Indian Ocean.  The probably fictitious sailor <a href="/wiki/Hippalus" title="Hippalus">Hippalus</a> is said to have learnt the direct route from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> to India around this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-24">[24]</a></sup>  During the 1st and 2nd centuries AD intensive <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">trade relations</a> developed between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamil_people" title="Tamil people">Tamil kingdoms</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chera_Dynasty" title="Chera Dynasty">Cheras</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chola_Dynasty" title="Chola Dynasty">Cholas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandyas" title="Pandyas">Pandyas</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">Southern India</a>. Like the Indonesian peoples above, the western sailors used the monsoon to cross the ocean. The unknown author of the <i><a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a></i> describes this route, as well as the commodities that were traded along various commercial ports on the coasts of the Horn of Africa and India circa 1&#160;CE. Among these trading settlements were <a href="/wiki/Mosylon" title="Mosylon">Mosylon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opone" title="Opone">Opone</a> on the Red Sea littoral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike the Pacific Ocean where the civilization of the <a href="/wiki/Polynesians" title="Polynesians">Polynesians</a> reached most of the far flung islands and atolls and populated them, almost all the islands, archipelagos and atolls of the Indian Ocean were uninhabited until colonial times. Although there were numerous ancient civilizations in the coastal states of Asia and parts of Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Maldives" title="Maldives">Maldives</a> were the only island group in the Central Indian Ocean region where an ancient civilization flourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO2004_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO2004-26">[26]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhivehi_people" title="Dhivehi people">Maldivian</a> ships used the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Monsoon_Current" title="Indian Monsoon Current">Indian Monsoon Current</a> to travel to the nearby coasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Romero_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Romero-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1405 to 1433 admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> said to have led large fleets of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> on several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">treasure voyages</a> through the Indian Ocean, ultimately reaching the coastal countries of <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dreyer_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dreyer-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1497 Portuguese navigator <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> rounded the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> and became the first European to sail to India (with the help of an Indian sailor) and later the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>. The European ships, armed with heavy cannon, quickly dominated trade. <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> achieved pre-eminence by setting up forts at the important straits and ports. Their hegemony along the coasts of Africa and Asia lasted until the mid 17th century. Later, the Portuguese were challenged by other European powers.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> (1602&#8211;1798) sought control of trade with the East across the Indian Ocean. <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> and Britain established trade companies for the area. From 1565 <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> established a major trading operation with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manila_Galleons" title="Manila Galleons">Manila Galleons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_East_Indies" title="Spanish East Indies">Pacific</a>. Spanish trading ships purposely avoided the Indian Ocean, following the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a> with Portugal. By 1815, Britain became the principal power in the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span></h3>\n<p>The opening of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> in 1869 revived European interest in the East, but no nation was successful in establishing trade dominance. Since <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> the United Kingdom was forced to withdraw from the area, to be replaced by India, the USSR, and the United States. The last two tried to establish hegemony by negotiating for naval base sites. Developing countries bordering the ocean, however, seek to have it made a "zone of peace" so that they may use its shipping lanes freely. The United Kingdom and United States maintain a military base on <a href="/wiki/Diego_Garcia" title="Diego Garcia">Diego Garcia</a> atoll in the middle of the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oceanographer-2001_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-oceanographer-2001-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 26 December 2004 fourteen countries around the Indian Ocean were hit by a wave of <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a> caused by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake">2004 Indian Ocean earthquake</a>. The waves radiated across the ocean at speeds exceeding 500&#160;km/h (310&#160;mph), reached up to 20&#160;m (66&#160;ft) in height, and resulted in an estimated 236,000 death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 2000s the ocean evolved into a hub of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piracy_in_Somalia" title="Piracy in Somalia">pirate</a> activity. By 2013, attacks off the Horn region\'s coast had steadily declined due to active private security and international navy patrols, especially by the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Navy" title="Indian Navy">Indian Navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wapstoas_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wapstoas-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malaysian_Airlines_Flight_370" title="Malaysian Airlines Flight 370">Malaysian Airlines Flight 370</a>, a Boeing 777 airliner with 239 persons on board, disappeared on 8 March 2014 and is alleged to have crashed into the southeastern Indian Ocean about 2,000&#160;km (1,200&#160;mi) from the coast of southwest <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>. Despite an extensive search, the whereabouts of the remains of the aircraft are unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade">Trade</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a></div><p>\nThe sea lanes in the Indian Ocean are considered among the most strategically important in the world with more than 80 percent of the world\'s seaborne trade in oil transits through Indian Ocean and its vital choke points, with 40 percent passing through the Strait of Hormuz, 35 percent through the Strait of Malacca and 8 percent through the Bab el-Mandab Strait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lamu_dhow_5.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lamu_dhow_5.JPG/220px-Lamu_dhow_5.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lamu_dhow_5.JPG/330px-Lamu_dhow_5.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lamu_dhow_5.JPG/440px-Lamu_dhow_5.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lamu_dhow_5.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Dhow" title="Dhow">dhow</a> off the coast of Kenya</div></div></div>\n<p>The Indian Ocean provides major sea routes connecting the Middle East, Africa, and East Asia with Europe and the Americas. It carries a particularly heavy traffic of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and petroleum products from the oil fields of the Persian Gulf and Indonesia. Large reserves of hydrocarbons are being tapped in the offshore areas of Saudi Arabia, Iran, India, and Western Australia. An estimated 40% of the world\'s offshore oil production comes from the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAWFB-1994_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-CIAWFB-1994-2">[2]</a></sup> Beach sands rich in heavy <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>, and offshore placer deposits are actively exploited by bordering countries, particularly India, Pakistan, South Africa, Indonesia, Sri Lanka, and Thailand.\n</p><p>Chinese companies have made investments in several Indian Ocean ports, including <a href="/wiki/Gwadar" title="Gwadar">Gwadar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hambantota" title="Hambantota">Hambantota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonadia" title="Sonadia">Sonadia</a>.  This has sparked a debate about the strategic implications of these investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brewster_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brewster-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span
